A line in the plane is called \\emph{sunny} if it is not parallel to any of the $x$-axis, the $y$-axis, or the line $x+y=0$.

Let $n \ge 3$ be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers $k$ such that there exist $n$ distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:
\begin{itemize}
    \item for all positive integers $a$ and $b$ with $a+b\le n+1$, the point $(a,b)$ lies on at least one of the lines; and
    \item exactly $k$ of the $n$ lines are sunny.
\end{itemize}

Respond in EXACTLY this format:

Let's analyze more generally. The set of points is the integer lattice triangle T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. Total points = n(n+1)/2.

We need to cover T_n with n distinct lines. The lines can be any lines. We want exactly k of them to be sunny (i.e., not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0). So the other n-k lines are non-sunny: they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1.

Observation: The triangle T_n has the property that all points lie on lines of slope -1 (diagonals) and also on rows and columns. In fact, the points can be partitioned into n rows (by y-coordinate) each with n+1-y points, and also n columns (by x-coordinate) each with n+1-x points, and also n diagonals (by sum) each with n+1-c points? Actually sum s = a+b ranges from 2 to n+1; diagonal with sum s has points with a from 1 to s-1, b = s-a, so s-1 points. So there are n diagonals (s=2,...,n+1). But note: each point lies on exactly one diagonal, one row, and one column.

Now, if we use a non-sunny line, it can be either horizontal (row), vertical (column), or diagonal (slope -1). Each such line covers all points on that line (which are a set of lattice points). So the union of some horizontal lines covers a set of points that are all points with y-coordinate in some set; similarly for vertical lines; diagonal lines cover points with sum in some set.

If we use sunny lines, they can be any other lines. They might also contain many points, but not necessarily all points on a given line; they can pass through at most some number of lattice points from T_n.

We have n lines total. The problem asks: For n≥3, determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines covering T_n, with exactly k sunny lines.

We need to find a necessary condition on k and then show that for all k satisfying the condition, we can construct such a set.

From the small cases, it appears that k can be 0,1,3 for n=3,4,5. Is k=2 ever possible? Let's try n=6 manually or logically.

First, note that the maximum number of points on a non-sunny line is n (e.g., row y=1). The maximum number of points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2) (since line y=x gives points (1,1),...,(floor((n+1)/2), floor((n+1)/2))). For n=6, floor(7/2)=3, so a sunny line can contain at most 3 points. So if we have many sunny lines, each covers at most 3 points. If we have few sunny lines, the non-sunny lines can cover many points, but we only have n lines total.

We need to cover 21 points with 6 lines. Let's try to see if we can have k=2. That means 2 sunny lines and 4 non-sunny lines. With 4 non-sunny lines, what is the maximum number of distinct points they can cover? They can be horizontal lines: y=1 (6 points), y=2 (5), y=3 (4), y=4 (3) covering 6+5+4+3=18 points. That leaves 3 points: (1,5),(1,6),(2,5)? Actually y=5 has (1,5),(2,5) sum=6? For n=6, a+b≤7: points with y=5: a=1,2 (since 1+5=6,2+5=7); y=6: a=1 only (1+6=7). So after y=1..4, uncovered points: (1,5),(2,5),(1,6). That's 3 points. Can we cover these with 2 sunny lines? Possibly one sunny line covers all three if they are collinear on a sunny line. Check collinearity: (1,5),(2,5) horizontal y=5, not sunny. (1,5),(1,6) vertical x=1, not sunny. (2,5),(1,6) slope (6-5)/(1-2)=1/(-1)=-1, line x+y=6? (2+5=7,1+6=7), so slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny triple. Could we use a sunny line covering two of them, and the other sunny line covering the remaining one? That would require each sunny line to cover at most 2 points, but we have 3 points to cover, so we need at least 2 sunny lines, that's fine. However, we need to cover all points. So if we can pair, say, (1,5) and (2,5) with a sunny line? That's horizontal, not sunny. (1,5) and (1,6) vertical. (2,5) and (1,6) slope -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair among these three points. So we cannot cover these three points with 2 sunny lines because any line through two of them is non-sunny. But could we cover them with one sunny line that also covers some other points? That would require that the three points are collinear on a sunny line, which they are not. So k=2 fails for n=6 with that selection.

Maybe choose different non-sunny lines: perhaps use y=1, y=2, y=3, and x=1? That covers: y=1:6, y=2:5, y=3:4, x=1:6. Overlap: y=1 and x=1 share (1,1); y=2 and x=1 share (1,2); y=3 and x=1 share (1,3). So distinct points: 6+5+4+6 -3 =18 points? Actually 6+5+4=15, plus 6 gives 21, minus 3 overlaps =18. So 18 points covered, leaving 3 points: (2,4),(2,5),(3,4)? Wait, list all points for n=6: a=1: b=1..6; a=2: b=1..5; a=3: b=1..4; a=4: b=1..3; a=5: b=1..2; a=6: b=1. So points: (1,1)-(1,6); (2,1)-(2,5); (3,1)-(3,4); (4,1)-(4,3); (5,1)-(5,2); (6,1). Remove y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1) removed. Remove x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(1,6) removed, but (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) already removed, so new: (1,4),(1,5),(1,6). So removed: all points except (2,4),(2,5),(3,4),(4,2)? For instance, maybe using rows1-4 and two diagonals? That would give union size? Let's compute for n=8: rows1-4: 8+7+6+5=26; add diagonal sum=7 (6 points) and sum=8 (7 points)? Overlaps: rows1-4 cover y=1..4; diagonal sum7: points with a+b=7: (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1). Overlaps with rows: row1 (6,1); row2 (5,2); row3 (4,3); row4 (3,4). So 4 overlaps. So union after adding sum7: 26+6-4=28. Then add sum8: points (1,7),(2,6),(3,5),(4,4),(5,3),(6,2),(7,1). Overlaps with rows1-4: row1 (7,1) already? row1 includes (7,1) because y=1, a=7; row2 (6,2) row2 has b=2, a=6; row3 (5,3) row3 has b=3, a=5; row4 (4,4) row4 has b=4, a=4. So 4 overlaps. Also overlaps with sum7: (1,7) not in sum7; (2,6) not; (3,5) not; (4,4) in sum7? sum7 has (4,3) not (4,4); (5,3) not; (6,2) not; (7,1) in sum7? sum7 has (6,1) not (7,1). So no overlap with sum7? Actually check (4,4) is in sum8 and also in sum7? sum7: (4,3) not; (5,3) in sum8 and sum7? sum7 has (5,2); no. So no overlap. So union after adding sum8: 28+7-4=31. Not 33. So not.

Thus, to get 33, we need the 6 lines to be exactly the 6 largest from one family. So complement is fixed. Therefore, k=2 impossible for n=8.

For n=9, similarly, the only way to get 42 points is rows1-7, leaving triple not sunny. Could we get 41 points? Then uncovered 4 points. Possibly those 4 points could be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we need to see if there is a selection of 7 lines that covers 41 points and leaves a set of 4 points that are on two sunny lines. For n=9, total points 45. With 7 lines, max union 42. So we could have 41, 42, etc. Let's try to find a selection that covers 41 points. For instance, take rows1-6: 9+8+7+6+5+4=33; add a diagonal that covers 8 points? That would be 41, but we need to ensure distinct. Rows1-6 cover points with y=1..6. The uncovered points: y=7: (1,7),(2,7); y=8: (1,8); y=9: (1,9). That's 4 points. Adding a diagonal could cover some of these. For example, diagonal sum=8: points (1,7),(2,6),(3,5),(4,4),(5,3),(6,2),(7,1). Among these, (1,7) is in uncovered; (2,6) is in rows1-6? row6 has b=6, a=1..3? Actually for n=9, rows: y=6: a=1..3? a+b≤10, b=6 => a≤4, so row6 has 4 points: (1,6),(2,6),(3,6),(4,6). So (2,6) is in row6, already covered. Similarly, (3,5) in row5, etc. So only (1,7) is new. So diagonal sum=8 adds 1 new point, making union 34, not 41. That's too small. To get 41, we need to cover 8 more points? Actually rows1-6 give 33; we need to add 8 points to get 41, but we only have 4 uncovered points, so we need to add a line that covers 4 new points and also maybe some already covered points? But a line can cover at most 9 points from T_9 (row1 has 9). The uncovered set has 4 points; to cover them all, we would need a line that passes through all 4. But those 4 points are (1,7),(2,7),(1,8),(1,9). Are they collinear? Check slopes: (1,7)-(2,7) horizontal; (1,7)-(1,8) vertical; (1,7)-(1,9) vertical; (2,7)-(1,8) slope (8-7)/(1-2)=1/(-1)=-1, line x+y=9? (2+7=9,1+8=9), slope -1; (2,7)-(1,9) slope (9-7)/(1-2)=2/(-1)=-2, line y=-2x+9? at x=1 gives y=7, at x=2 gives y=5, not (2,7). So no line contains all 4. So cannot cover all 4 with one line. Could we cover them with two lines? That would require two sunny lines, but we have only 2 sunny lines total; if we use one of them to cover some of these points, the other sunny line can cover the rest. But we already have 7 non-sunny lines; we need to select 7 non-sunny lines such that the uncovered set is, say, 4 points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The uncovered set after using rows1-6 is exactly 4 points; we need to see if we can cover those 4 points with 2 sunny lines. As we saw, the only sunny pairs among these 4 are (1,7)-(2,7) horizontal not; (1,7)-(1,8) vertical not; (1,7)-(1,9) vertical not; (2,7)-(1,8) slope -1 not; (2,7)-(1,9) slope -2 sunny? (1,9) to (2,7): slope (7-9)/(2-1)= -2, so line y=-2x+11? At x=1 gives y=9; at x=2 gives y=7; so (2,7) and (1,9) are on line y=-2x+11 (sunny). Also (2,7)-(1,8) is slope -1, not sunny. So we have one sunny pair: (2,7)-(1,9). Then remaining (1,7) and (1,8) are vertical, not sunny. So cannot cover with 2 sunny lines.

What about using rows1-5 and two diagonals? Rows1-5: 9+8+7+6+5=35; uncovered: rows6-9: y=6:4 points (1,6)-(4,6); y=7:3 points (1,7)-(3,7); y=8:2 points (1,8),(2,8); y=9:1 point (1,9). That's 4+3+2+1=10 points. To cover 35, we need to cover 10 more points with 2 lines. That's impossible because each line covers at most 9. So not.

Thus, for n=9, k=2 seems impossible. So far, pattern: k=2 is impossible for n=3..9. Could it be impossible for all n? Let's try to prove that for n≥3, it's impossible to have exactly 2 sunny lines.

We need to show that any covering of T_n by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines cannot exist. Equivalently, we need to show that if we have n lines covering T_n, and exactly 2 of them are sunny, then something forces a contradiction. Perhaps we can derive a lower bound on the number of non-sunny lines needed, or use an invariant.

Observation: Non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Each such line contains a set of lattice points. The points of T_n can be partitioned into n rows (by y), n columns (by x), n diagonals (by sum). The rows, columns, diagonals are all families of non-sunny lines. The problem is about covering with n lines, but we have flexibility.

Maybe we can consider the sum of the coordinates of points. For each point (a,b), a+b = s. The lines with slope -1 have equation x+y = constant. A line with slope 1 (sunny) has equation y = x + c. The slope 1 lines can contain many points if c is small. In fact, the maximum number of points on a sunny line is floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, floor(4/2)=2; n=4, floor(5/2)=2; n=5, floor(6/2)=3; n=6, floor(7/2)=3; n=7, floor(8/2)=4; etc.

Now, if we have exactly 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2*floor((n+1)/2) points, but they may also cover points that are already covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are at most n-2 lines. The total number of points is n(n+1)/2. So we need the union of the n lines to be T_n. If we have only 2 sunny lines, then the non-sunny lines must cover all but the points covered by the sunny lines. But the sunny lines can cover at most 2*floor((n+1)/2) points. So the non-sunny lines must cover at least n(n+1)/2 - 2*floor((n+1)/2) points. But each non-sunny line can cover at most n points (if it is a row with y=1, or column with x=1, or diagonal with sum=n+1). So with n-2 non-sunny lines, the maximum they can cover is (n-2)*n. But we need to cover at least n(n+1)/2 - 2*floor((n+1)/2) points. Let's compare: (n-2)n = n^2 - 2n; n(n+1)/2 - 2*floor((n+1)/2). For large n, floor((n+1)/2) ≈ n/2, so RHS ≈ n^2/2 - n = n^2/2 - n. LHS = n^2 - 2n, which is larger. So the bound does not preclude. But we need to consider overlaps. The non-sunny lines might not be disjoint; they could overlap a lot, so the union size could be less than n^2 - 2n. In fact, the maximum union from n-2 non-sunny lines is n(n+1)/2 - 3, as we argued. So they can cover at most n(n+1)/2 - 3 points. So the sunny lines must cover the remaining at least 3 points. But we have 2 sunny lines; they could cover many points, but the missing points are exactly the complement of the non-sunny lines. So we need to see if the complement (which has size at least 3) can be contained in the union of 2 sunny lines. Since the complement is the set of points not covered by the non-sunny lines, and those points are exactly the points that are not on any of the selected non-sunny lines. So we need to find a family of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the set of points not on any of them is covered by 2 sunny lines. We've seen that if we take the n-2 largest rows, the complement is a triple that is not covered by 2 sunny lines. Could there be another selection of n-2 non-sunny lines that gives a complement of size 3 that is on a sunny line? For that, we would need to find a set of n-2 lines that cover all but a triple of points that lie on a line with slope 1 (or some other sunny slope). Since the maximum union from n-2 lines is n(n+1)/2 - 3, to get a complement of size 3, we need to achieve the maximum union. So we need to know if there exists a set of n-2 non-sunny lines whose union is exactly T_n minus a triple that is on a sunny line. The maximum union is achieved by any family of n-2 lines that are pairwise disjoint and each has the largest possible size. The largest disjoint families are rows1..n-2, columns1..n-2, and diagonals from 4 to n+1? Actually the largest sizes: rows y=1 gives n points, y=2 gives n-1, ..., y=n gives 1. The sum of the n-2 largest rows is n + (n-1) + ... + 3 = n(n+1)/2 - 3. Similarly for columns. For diagonals, the sizes are 1,2,...,n. The sum of the n-2 largest diagonals is n + (n-1) + ... + 3 = same. So these families achieve the maximum union. Are there any other families of n-2 lines that also achieve the maximum union? Possibly families that include lines of different types but still have disjoint sets? Since rows, columns, diagonals are all disjoint families? They are not necessarily disjoint across families; but to achieve the maximum union, we need the sets to be disjoint and each line to have the maximum possible size. The only lines that have size n are rows y=1, columns x=1, and diagonals with sum=n+1? Actually diagonal sum=n+1 has n points (a=1..n,b=n+1-a). So there are three lines of size n. But any two of these lines intersect at a point? Row y=1 and column x=1 intersect at (1,1); row y=1 and diagonal sum=n+1 intersect at (n,1) if n+1= sum? Actually (n,1) sum=n+1, so yes; column x=1 and diagonal sum=n+1 intersect at (1,n) because 1+n = n+1; so they are not disjoint. So to get disjoint lines, we cannot use more than one from each family? Actually the rows are pairwise disjoint; columns are pairwise disjoint; diagonals are pairwise disjoint. So the maximum disjoint families are exactly rows, columns, or diagonals. So the only ways to achieve the maximum union of n-2 lines are to take the n-2 rows with largest sizes, or the n-2 columns, or the n-2 diagonals with largest sizes. All these give a complement of size 3. For rows, the complement is the three points: (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n). For columns, complement: (n-1,1),(n,1),(n-1,2)? Let's compute: columns x=1..n-2: x=1 gives n points, x=2 gives n-1, ... x=n-2 gives 3 points. Uncovered: x=n-1 and x=n. For n=9, columns: x=1..7 cover; uncovered: x=8 (points a=8,b≤2? Actually a=8,b≤2 because a+b≤10, b≤2; so points (8,1),(8,2); and x=9: (9,1) only. So triple = {(8,1),(8,2),(9,1)}. For rows, triple = {(1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n)}. For diagonals, complement: sums 2,3: (1,1); sum2: (1,1); sum3: (1,2),(2,1). So triple = {(1,1),(1,2),(2,1)}. In all cases, the complement is not on a sunny line. Could it be that for some n, one of these triples is on a sunny line? Check for n=3: rows1-1? n-2=1, rows1: y=1, points (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). Uncovered: y=2: (1,2),(2,2) but n=3, a+b≤4: row2 has a=1,2? Actually for n=3, rows: y=1:3, y=2:2, y=3:1. Rows1 covers 3 points, leaves row2 and row3: (1,2),(2,2) and (1,3). That's 3 points: (1,2),(2,2),(1,3). Is that triple sunny? Check slopes: (1,2)-(2,2) horizontal; (1,2)-(1,3) vertical; (2,2)-(1,3) slope (3-2)/(1-2)=1/(-1)=-1, not sunny. So no. For n=4: rows1-2: rows1 (4), row2 (3) cover 7 points; leaves row3 (2 points) and row4 (1 point): (1,3),(2,3) and (1,4) => triple (1,3),(2,3),(1,4). Check sunny: (1,3)-(2,3) horizontal, no; (1,3)-(1,4) vertical; (2,3)-(1,4) slope (4-3)/(1-2)=1/(-1)=-1, no. For n=5: rows1-3: 5+4+3=12 points; leaves row4 (2 points) and row5 (1 point): (1,4),(2,4) and (1,5) => triple (1,4),(2,4),(1,5). Check: (1,4)-(2,4) horizontal; (1,4)-(1,5) vertical; (2,4)-(1,5) slope (5-4)/(1-2)=1/(-1)=-1, no. So never sunny. For columns similar. For diagonals, complement triple (1,1),(1,2),(2,1) is never on a sunny line (as checked). So indeed, the complement from achieving the maximum union is never sunny. Therefore, any set of n-2 non-sunny lines that achieves the maximum union cannot be used with 2 sunny lines to cover the missing points. What if we use a set of n-2 non-sunny lines that does not achieve the maximum union, so that the complement has size >3? Then we need to cover more than 3 points with 2 sunny lines. That might be possible if the complement is, say, 4 points that are on two sunny lines (each covering 2 points). But then the union of the non-sunny lines is smaller than the maximum, so the total points covered by non-sunny lines is less than n(n+1)/2 - 3. That means the non-sunny lines are not covering all points; they leave a larger set. Then we need to cover that larger set with 2 sunny lines. However, each sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points, so two sunny lines can cover at most 2*floor((n+1)/2) points. For n large, that is about n points. So covering a set of size, say, 4 is fine. But we also need to ensure that the non-sunny lines themselves are distinct and not sunny; they are allowed to be any non-sunny lines. So we need to see if we can choose n-2 non-sunny lines such that the uncovered set is of size exactly 4, and those 4 points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. And also the union of the non-sunny lines must be disjoint from the sunny lines? Not necessarily, but the sunny lines cover the uncovered points; they might also cover some points already covered by non-sunny lines, but that's okay.

So the question reduces to: For n≥3, does there exist a set of n-2 non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) such that the complement (points not on any of them) is a set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines? And also the total lines (n-2 non-sunny + 2 sunny) must be distinct. This is a combinatorial design problem.

We have seen that for n=6, the only way to get complement size 3 is to use rows1-4, which leaves a triple not coverable by 2 sunny lines. Could we get complement size 4? For n=6, total points 21. With 4 non-sunny lines, the maximum union is 18, so the complement size is at least 3. If we can achieve union 17, complement size 4. Is there a selection of 4 non-sunny lines that covers exactly 17 points? We need to see if we can get 17. Possibly using rows y=1,2,3,4 gives 18; rows y=1,2,3,5 gives 6+5+4+2=17. So take rows y=1,2,3,5. That's 4 lines. Then uncovered points: row4 (a=1..3, b=4? (1,6) and (2,4): slope (4-6)/(2-1)=-2, line y=-2x+8, at x=1 gives y=6, at x=2 gives y=4, so yes (1,6) and (2,4) are on line y=-2x+8, slope -2 sunny. So that line covers (1,6) and (2,4). Then we have left (1,4) and (3,4). Those two are on horizontal y=4, not sunny; could we cover them with a sunny line? (1,4)-(3,4) horizontal; no. Could we use a different sunny line? Perhaps one line covers (1,4),(2,4),(3,4) horizontal, not sunny; so no. Could we use a line through (1,4) and (1,6) vertical, not sunny; through (2,4) and (1,6) already considered; through (3,4) and (1,6) slope (6-4)/(1-3)=2/(-2)=-1, not sunny; through (1,4) and (3,4) horizontal; through (2,4) and (3,4) horizontal. So only one sunny pair. Then the remaining two points are horizontal, not sunny. So not.

What about rows y=1,2,4,5? That's 6+5+3+2=16, leaving 5 points. Not.

Thus, for n=6, no selection of 4 non-sunny lines gives a coverable complement. So k=2 impossible.

For n=7, we tried rows1-5 (25 points) leaving triple; rows1-4 and row6? That's 7+6+5+4+2=24, leaving 4 points: row5 (3 points) and row7 (1 point) => {(1,5),(2,5),(3,5),(1,7)}. What about rows1-4 and diagonal sum=8? That would be 7+6+5+4=22, plus sum8 (7 points) overlaps? sum8: points (1,7),(2,6),(3,5),(4,4),(5,3),(6,2),(7,1). Given the pattern, perhaps k=2 is impossible for all n. But we need to confirm for larger n. Let's try to prove that any covering of T_n by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines cannot exist. Possibly by using an invariant: the sum of the coordinates of points on sunny lines must be something.

Alternatively, we can consider the complement of the non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Each such line covers a set of points. The points not covered by these lines must be covered by the sunny lines. Since we have exactly 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2*floor((n+1)/2) points. So the number of points not covered by the non-sunny lines must be ≤ 2*floor((n+1)/2). But we also have that the non-sunny lines cover at most n(n+1)/2 - 3 points (as we argued). So the complement size is at least 3. For n large, 2*floor((n+1)/2) is about n, so the complement could be up to n. So that doesn't give contradiction.

Maybe we can show that the complement of n-2 non-sunny lines cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because of some parity or collinearity condition. Let's try to analyze the structure of the complement.

The non-sunny lines are from families: rows, columns, diagonals. Suppose we have a set of n-2 such lines. Their union covers some points. The points not covered are those that are not on any of these lines. In particular, consider the set of points with a given y-coordinate (row). If we have a row line covering that y, then all points in that row are covered. If not, then points in that row may be covered by columns or diagonals, but some may remain uncovered. Similarly for columns and diagonals.

The sunny lines we will use to cover the uncovered points must be such that they do not pass through any of the covered points? Not necessarily, but they can. However, if we have two sunny lines, they will each be some lines of slope not 0,∞,-1. They will contain some lattice points from T_n. The uncovered points must be among those lattice points. So we need to find a set of n-2 lines from the three families that leave a set of points that is contained in the union of two lines of other slopes.

But we need to check if a covering exists. This is getting too deep.

Given the complexity, I suspect the problem has a known solution: The answer is all k from 0 to n except 2. However, we have counterexamples for n=5 and n=6 where k=4 and k=5 are not possible. So maybe the answer is: k can be 0,1,3 for all n≥3, and also k=n-1 when n is even? For n=4, n-1=3 works; n=6, n-1=5 does not work; n=8, maybe works? Let's test n=6: we had k=5 not possible. So not.

Let's compute the possible k for small n using known results. Perhaps we can find a general construction.

Observation: The set of points T_n can be covered by n lines in many ways. The simplest is using all n horizontal lines, which are all non-sunny (k=0). Also using all n vertical lines gives k=0. Using all n diagonals (x+y=constant) gives k=0. So k=0 is always possible.

Now, we want to have exactly k sunny lines. For k=1, we can use one sunny line and n-1 non-sunny lines. For instance, use rows y=1..n-1 (n-1 lines) covering all points except those with y=n. Those points are (1,n), (2,n) if sum≤n+1? Actually for n≥3, y=n has only point (1,n) because a+b≤n+1, b=n => a≤1; so only (1,n). Then we need a sunny line through (1,n). That is possible: take line y = x + (n-1) (slope1) passes through (1,n) and (2,n+1) not; but also through (1,n) alone; that line is sunny. So k=1 works. Similarly, we can use columns x=1..n-1 covering all except (n,1); then use a sunny line through (n,1). So k=1 works.

Now, for k=2, we need to use 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines must cover all points that are not covered by the sunny lines. The union of the non-sunny lines is at most n(n+1)/2 - 3, as argued. So the complement has size at least 3. For the 2 sunny lines to cover that complement, they must be able to cover at least 3 points. But can they cover exactly 3 points that are the complement of some family of n-2 non-sunny lines? We need to find a set of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the uncovered set is covered by 2 sunny lines. The uncovered set size must be at most 2*floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, floor(4/2)=2, so at most 4; complement size 3, possible. For n=3, we tried rows1-1 (y=1) gives complement (1,2),(2,2),(1,3) which is 3 points; can we cover these with 2 sunny lines? We already saw no. For n=4, we tried rows1-2 gives complement (1,3),(2,3),(1,4) - 3 points; no. For n=5, rows1-3 gives complement (1,4),(2,4),(1,5) - 3 points; no. For n=6, rows1-4 gives complement (1,5),(2,5),(1,6) - 3 points; no. For n=7, rows1-5 gives complement (1,6),(2,6),(1,7) - 3 points; no. So the family of rows1..n-2 always gives a complement that is not coverable by 2 sunny lines. But could there be another family of n-2 non-sunny lines that gives a different complement of size 3 that is coverable? The complement size is 3 only if the non-sunny lines achieve the maximum union (n(n+1)/2 - 3). So we need to consider all families of n-2 lines from the three families that give union of size n(n+1)/2 - 3. Those families are exactly the sets of n-2 rows, n-2 columns, or n-2 diagonals with largest sizes. The complements of these are as we listed: rows complement: {(1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n)}; columns complement: {(n-1,1),(n,1),(n-1,2)}; diagonals complement: {(1,1),(1,2),(2,1)}. None of these are on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1? Check: rows complement: (1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n). Are these collinear on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1? They are not collinear at all; they form a right triangle. So no line passes through all three. Could they be covered by two lines? For example, one line through (1,n-1) and (2,n-1) is horizontal (non-sunny); the other through (1,n-1) and (1,n) is vertical (non-sunny); so not. Could we use a sunny line through (1,n-1) and (2,n-1) is horizontal not; through (1,n-1) and (1,n) is vertical not; through (2,n-1) and (1,n) slope? (n-(n-1))/(1-2)=1/(-1)=-1, line x+y = n+1? (2,n-1)+(1,n)=2+n-1= n+1? Actually (2,n-1) sum = n+1, (1,n) sum = n+1, so line x+y = n+1, slope -1, not sunny. So no. Thus, not coverable by 2 sunny lines.

For columns complement: (n-1,1),(n,1),(n-1,2). Similarly, not coverable.

For diagonals complement: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). Check if they lie on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1: (1,1)-(1,2) vertical; (1,1)-(2,1) horizontal; (1,2)-(2,1) slope -1, line x+y=3? Actually (1+2=3,2+1=3) so slope -1. So no sunny line passes through all three. Could they be covered by two sunny lines? For instance, one line through (1,1) and (1,2) is vertical not sunny; through (1,1) and (2,1) horizontal not; through (1,2) and (2,1) slope -1 not sunny. So no.

Thus, for any n≥3, the complement of any set of n-2 non-sunny lines that achieves the maximum union cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. What about sets that achieve a smaller union? Then the complement size is larger than 3. For example, we could use n-3 rows and one more line to get complement size maybe 4 or 5. For n=7, we saw that using rows1-4 and row6 gave complement 4 points, which were not coverable by 2 sunny lines. Could there be a selection that gives complement size 4 that is coverable by 2 sunny lines? For n=7, total points 28. To get complement size 4, we need union 24. Is there a family of 5 non-sunny lines that covers 24 points? Possibly rows1-4 and row6 gave 24, complement 4 points: (1,5),(2,5),(3,5),(1,7). We already saw that set is not coverable by 2 sunny lines. What about rows1-5 and column1? That gives union 25? Actually rows1-5 cover 25 points; column1 adds 7 points but overlaps with rows1-5 at 5 points? So union =25+7-5=27; complement size 1 point: (1,7) not covered? Actually (1,7) is in column1 but not in rows1-5? rows1-5 cover y=1..5, so (1,7) is not covered by rows; but it is covered by column1? column1 includes (1,7); so (1,7) is covered by column1. Wait, we need 5 non-sunny lines for k=2, but here we have 6 non-sunny lines? Actually we are considering n=7, k=2 means we have 2 sunny lines and 5 non-sunny lines. Thus, for k=2, we need exactly 5 non-sunny lines. So we need a set of 5 lines covering 24 points (since total 28, complement 4). So uncovered: (2,5),(3,5),(1,6),(2,6),(1,7),(2,7?) Actually (2,7) sum=9>8 no; (1,6) sum=7; (2,6) sum=8; (1,7) sum=8; (3,5) sum=8; (2,5) sum=7; (3,5) sum=8; (1,5) covered; (4,5) no; (5,5) no. Thus, only rows1-4 and row6 gives complement size 4. Similarly, columns1-4 and column6 gives complement size 4. Are there any other combinations of 5 non-sunny lines that cover exactly 24 points? Possibly using a mix of rows and diagonals? For n=7, we need to cover 28 points with 5 lines. The maximum union from 5 lines is 25 (rows1-5, cols1-5, diag4-8). Thus, for n=7, the only families of 5 non-sunny lines that cover 24 points are those that give complement size 4, and we've seen that complement is not coverable by 2 sunny lines. Could there be a set of 5 non-sunny lines that covers 23 points, leaving 5 points, and those 5 points might be coverable by 2 sunny lines? 2 sunny lines can cover at most 8 points, but we need to cover 5 points; it's possible if the 5 points can be covered by two lines. Wait, rows1-4=22, sum8=7, overlaps=4, union=25; that's max. So not.

What about rows1-4 and column1? That gave 25. So no.

What about rows1-3 and columns1-2? That's 5 lines, union=25, complement 3 points.

What about rows1-3 and column1 and diagonal? That's 5 lines? Actually rows1-3 (3 lines), column1 (1), diagonal sum=5? That's 5 lines. Actually row2 has b=2, so (2,3) not; (3,2) in row2? row2 b=2, no; (1,4) in row4 not; (4,1) in row1? row1 b=1, yes (4,1) is in row1. Thus, seems for n=7, k=2 impossible.

For n=8, we need 6 non-sunny lines covering 36 points. The maximum union from 6 lines is 33 (rows1-6, cols1-6, diag4-9). So to cover 36 points, we need union 33, leaving 3 points. As before, those 3 points are not coverable by 2 sunny lines. If we try to cover 32 points, leaving 4 points, we need a set of 6 lines that covers 32 points. Thus, for n=7, k=2 seems impossible.

Given the pattern, it's plausible that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. Let's try to prove it.

We need to show that there is no covering of T_n by n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines. Equivalently, for any set of n-2 non-sunny lines, the set of points not covered by them cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines.

Maybe we can use a parity or counting argument. Consider the sum of the coordinates of points on sunny lines. Another approach: The non-sunny lines are of three types: horizontal, vertical, and diagonal (slope -1). Let's denote the family of non-sunny lines as a set of n-2 such lines. For each such line, the points it covers are all points on that line within T_n. The union of these lines is some set. The complement, which we need to cover with 2 sunny lines, must be a subset of the union of 2 sunny lines. Since each sunny line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points from T_n, the two sunny lines together can contain at most 2*floor((n+1)/2) points from T_n. But the complement size is at least 3. So it's possible.

We can try to show that the complement of any set of n-2 lines from the three families cannot be contained in the union of 2 lines of slope not 0,∞,-1. This might be a combinatorial geometry result.

Given the difficulty, perhaps the intended answer is that all k from 0 to n except 2 are possible. But we have counterexamples for n=5,6 where k=4,5 are not possible.
